Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Two-dimensional ordered superstructures have been attracting considerable attention due to their interesting properties and potential applications. However, designing ideal functional superstructures with excellent electrochemical properties is still a major challenge, and an in-depth understanding of the structure–activity relationship of electrodes remains to be achieved. To elucidate this critical issue, herein, we rationally designed and synthesized for the first time superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub>/dual-doped mesoporous carbon anodes using confined space and surface coassembly strategies. Our method primarily relied on the larger interlayer space few-layered MXene and its negatively charged surface, allowing hexamethylenetetramine intercalation and surface electrostatic adsorption. The superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub>/dual-doped mesoporous carbon was successfully assembled by the thermal decomposition of a confined carbon precursor. Subsequently, the comparison of Na<sup>+</sup>-storage properties of various anodes was carried out based on the results of structural characterization techniques and electrochemical analysis methods. The results showed that the optimized anode (N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20) can deliver a reversible capacity of 165 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating excellent electrochemical properties. The enhancement can be attributed to the synergistic effect of carbon domains, defective nanocrystals, and a covalently coupled interface between TiO<sub>2</sub> and mesoporous carbon. Our work not only offered a new strategy for the assembly and regulation of superstructures to promote the electrochemical performance but also enlightened the rational design of advanced anodes for sodium-ion battery application.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D8887%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DZilu%2BZhang%252C%2BMing%2BLiu%252C%2BYunyun%2BXie%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D61%26issueNum%3D23%26contentID%3Dacs.inorgchem.2c01009%26title%3DSuperstructured%2BNanocrystals%252FDual-Doped%2BMesoporous%2BCarbon%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BHigh-Performance%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D0020-1669%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dinocaj%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D8897%26publicationDate%3DJune%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291337&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon" class="article__tags__link">Carbon</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291138&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Superstructures" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Superstructures" class="article__tags__link">Superstructures</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div class="hlFld-Abstract synopsis"><h2>Synopsis</h2><span><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub>/dual-doped mesoporous carbon anodes are rationally designed and synthesized for the first time using confined space and surface coassembly strategies. The method primarily relies on the larger interlayer space few-layered MXene and its negatively charged surface, allowing hexamethylenetetramine intercalation and surface electrostatic adsorption. Our work not only offers a new strategy for the assembly and regulation of a superstructure to promote the electrochemical performance but also enlightens the rational design of advanced anodes for sodium-ion battery application.</p></span></div><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) is larger than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å) in radius, the diffusion kinetics of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is slower than that of lithium-ion batteries. To date, this presents a big challenge to prepare appropriate electrodes with high-rate performance for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> Previous studies show that the heterogeneous interface region of materials usually exhibited several orders of magnitude higher ionic and electronic conductivity than the bulk phase due to the electron delocalization effect, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> built-in electric field, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 7 to 9">(7−9)</a> and covalent coupling of chemical bonds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 10 to 12">(10−12)</a> These characteristics provide important guidance for the development of advanced anodes. One of the scientific problems is how to improve their sodium-storage properties and prepare new anodes by accurately designing and regulating the composition and structure of a heterogeneous interface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Theoretically, the atomic-level contact heterostructure interface of material/carbon has obvious advantages of active sites and ion-diffusion pathways as compared to traditional heterostructures, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 16 to 18">(16−18)</a> according to the electrochemical reaction mechanism (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>a,b). This is expected to achieve faster reaction kinetics and accelerate the ion-diffusion transport rates. It is well known that a 2D superlattice is composed of nanosheets stacked on each other at the atomic scale. Two single-layer nanosheets are isolated from each other, making it possible to make full use of each single-layer nanosheet. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 19 to 22">(19−22)</a> For example, Xiong et al. prepared alternately stacked MnO<sub>2</sub>/graphene superlattices, demonstrating the real monolayer MnO<sub>2</sub> nanowires for high-performance lithium and sodium storage. At the current density of 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, reversible specific capacities of 480 and 185 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, 20% of the initial capacity was maintained after 5000 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Li et al. synthesized poly(ethylene oxide)-intercalated MoS<sub>2</sub> composites (PEO-MoS<sub>2</sub>) by the peeling-stacking method and then used them as anode materials for SIBs. At the current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific capacity was 225 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, twice that of commercial MoS<sub>2</sub>, and the rate performance was improved by 21% due to the increase of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> In view of these, our work proposes a 2D superstructure-like (superstructured) material model with a guest carbon layer for SIB anodes.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. (a,b) Concept behind the Rational Design of a Superstructure for Creating an Ideal Atomic Interface to Enhance Na<sup>+</sup> Storage; (c) Schematic Indicating the Two-Step Interaction between HMT and MXenes</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">TiO<sub>2</sub> possesses a high theoretical capacity of 335 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, an appropriate insertion potential of sodium (∼0.6 V <i>vs</i> Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), excellent structural stability, and pseudocapacitance characteristics, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> showing its great potential as a SIB anode. At present, numerous studies have been very creative in the preparation of TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes at different scales. For example, doping at the atomic scale is an effective means to improve the driving force of charge transfer in TiO<sub>2</sub>; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> micro/mesoporous structures and carbon coating at the microstructure scale are conducive to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electron transfer; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 25, 29 and 30">(25,29,30)</a> and the construction of a macroporous conductive network can promote the penetration of electrolytes and the conductivity of the whole electrode at the macrostructure scale. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> Although these studies had made a series of progress, TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals still did not meet the level of “full exposure” redox activity (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>b). In this context, it is worth reconsidering the assembly and electrochemical properties of superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes. Nevertheless, there is little discussion on the fundamental issues related to the synthesis–structure–activity relationship of superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes as well as their capacity attenuation mechanism during the Na<sup>+</sup> extrusion–insertion process. Therefore, it is of great significance to study the preparation of a TiO<sub>2</sub>/C superstructure and its electrochemical reaction mechanism for realizing high-performance SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">MXenes have been proved to be a common material for the construction of organic/inorganic composites because their surface is easy to oxidize and they have a rich surface chemistry and a layered structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Considering the obvious advantages of hexamethylenetetramine (HMT) as a ligand for forming a 2D supramolecular structure, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> in this work, we choose MXenes as the symbiotic template and HMT as the ligand to construct a 2D superstructure, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> wherein HMT is proposed to be selected as the organic ligand. The main reason is that the terminal atom on the surface of the MXene has a low work function and high electronegativity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 and 35">(33,35)</a> As a strong electron acceptor, HMT is conducive to deprotonation and therefore it bridges with the metal atom below the MXene. In the meantime, the interlayer of few-layered MXenes can provide an intercalation space for cations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36,37)</a> Therefore, using MXenes as the symbiotic template, the preparation of inorganic/organic superlattice materials could be realized by surface electrostatic interaction and intercalation coassembly (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme 1</a>c). Benefiting from this unique superstructure, the optimized anode (N/OC@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20) can provide a reversible capacity of 165 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating its excellent Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The purpose of this paper is to maximize the nanocrystalline and carbon-coated interfacial contact. On the other hand, our work also provides a new idea for the structural design of conversion-type or alloy-type anodes in the future for solving the issues of capacity attenuation and instability caused by electrode pulverization. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 38 to 40">(38−40)</a></div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.1.  Synthesis of Few-Layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXenes</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Few-layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXenes were prepared by etching and stripping 2.0 g Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> MAX powder with a mixed solution of 9 mol L<sup>–1</sup> HCl (40 mL total) and 2.0 g LiF. The reaction mixture was then kept at 40 °C for 48 h, the etched powder was washed with deionized (DI) water, centrifuged at 3500 rpm for 5 min, and the supernatant was washed. These procedures were repeated several times until the pH of the supernatant reached ∼6. Finally, the suspension was freeze-dried to obtain few-layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> MXenes. The specific preparation process is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.2.  Synthesis of Zn-HMT</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Generally, 0.5 g HMT and 1.05 g Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were dissolved in 25 and 15 mL ethanol solution, respectively. Subsequently, Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O solution was added to HMT solution drop by drop, and a white precipitate was immediately generated, indicating the formation of Zn-HMT. Zn-HMT was obtained by centrifugation and washed several times with alcohol to remove any possible contaminants. Finally, the sample was dried in a vacuum oven at 60° C overnight. The detailed synthesis route is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a</a>.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3.  Preparation of N/O-Dual-Doped Carbon Material</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Zn-HMT powder was directly carbonized at 800 °C for 2 h at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup> under an Ar atmosphere. The corresponding N/O-dual-doped carbon materials were obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2e</a>). The as-obtained anodes were labeled as N/O-C.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.4.  Preparation of Superstructured N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-X Anodes</h3><div class="NLM_p">Different masses of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> (10 mg, 20 mg and 30 mg), HMT, and Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were added to the ethanol solvent, respectively. In all runs, only the amount of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> was changed. The amounts of HMT and Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were held constant. First, 0.5 g HMT, 0.02 g Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, and 1.05 g Zn (NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O were added to 25 mL ethanol solvent, respectively. Then, the above HMT solution was added to the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> suspension. After stirring for 30 min, the Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O solution was added to the mixed suspension of HMT/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> drop by drop. At the same time, gray flocs were observed. The precipitate was collected and dried in a vacuum oven at 60 °C overnight. Finally, Zn-HMT/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>-20 was directly carbonized at 800 °C for 2 h under an Ar atmosphere at a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, and the corresponding powders were labeled as N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-X (X represents different amounts of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a). For comparison, MXene-derived TiO<sub>2</sub> anodes were also obtained under the same temperature and atmospheric conditions.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Illustration of the synthetic route of superstructured N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; (b,c) FESEM images of superstructured Zn-HMT/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>-20; (d) XRD patterns of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>, Zn-HMT/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; and (e–g) FESEM image of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20. The inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f shows the layer-by-layer structure.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">2.5.  Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure information of all samples was obtained by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Panaco Holland, Cu Kα radiation, λ = 1.5406 Å). Both the carbon structure and Ti–O vibration information of the samples were analyzed by Raman spectroscopy (JY HR800 miniature Raman spectrometer, 532 nm excitation). The sample functional group and Ti–O vibration information was revealed by the FTIR spectra obtained on a Thermo Nicolet NEXUS 670. The optical band gap of the samples was measured using a UV–Vis spectrophotometer (Shimadzu UV 1700). The sample geometry information was obtained by field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM, JEOL JSM6300, Japan) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEOL, JEM-2010F). The specific surface area and pore size distribution of the samples were obtained by nitrogen adsorption at 77 K (Quantachrome, NOVA-1200e), respectively. Thermogravimetric (TG) analysis was carried out in air at a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>–1</sup> from room temperature to 800 °C using a thermal analyzer (STA 449F3, Netzsch, Germany).</div></div><div id="sec2_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">2.6.  Electrochemical Performance Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">For the preparation of the working electrode, 70 wt % of anode, 20 wt % of conductive agent, and 10 wt % of poly(vinylidene fluoride) binder were used, and the solvent used was <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone. The above raw materials were made into slurries and coated on a copper foil, and at 90° C vacuum drying was performed for 14 h. The abovementioned copper sheet was prepared into an electrode with a diameter of 14 mm by a tablet press, and the active material loading of the electrode was set to 0.9 mg. All the cells were prepared in an Ar-atmosphere glovebox employing 1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DMC/EMC = 1:1:1 V % as the electrolyte. The sodium flake was employed as a counter electrode. The rate performance and cycling stability of the battery were tested using an Xinwei test system (Shenzhen, China) at 25 °C, and cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed using the CHI760E electrochemical workstation (Shanghai Chenhua, China). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed in the range of 0.01 to 10<sup>5</sup> Hz. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was used to estimate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity during the intercalation process. GITT measurements were performed on a LANHE battery test system (Wuhan Blue River Electronics, China). The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> in the electrode was calculated using the following formula (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span>where τ is the pulse time; <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>V</i><sub>M</sub>, and <i>M</i><sub>B</sub> are the mass of the electrode (mg), the molar volume (cm<sup>3</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>), and the molar mass of the anodes (g mol<sup>–1</sup>), respectively; <i>S</i> is the geometric area of the electrode (cm<sup>3</sup>); Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub> is the potential difference (V); and Δ<i>E</i><sub>s</sub> is the potential difference.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The synthesis of few-layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>. By electrostatic adsorption and intercalation processes, we first prepared the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C superstructure by the heat treatment of the Zn-HMT/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> superstructure. The corresponding synthesis and structural characterization of superstructured materials are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a. Previous studies showed that ammonium salt can not only intercalate into the interlayer of MXene <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42 ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> but also adsorb on the surface of MXene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44" aria-label="reference 44">(44)</a> Moreover, a 2D supermolecular structure can also be formed by the HMT–nitrate interaction (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a–d</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref44" aria-label="reference 36, 37 and 44">(36,37,44)</a> It is emphasized that in the process of preparing superstructure materials, the reduced zinc can form a mesoporous structure by evaporation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Both the introduction of oxygen by Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and a large amount of N from HMT provided the reaction requirements for the formation of mesoporous N, O-dual-doped carbon and the oxidation of the Ti source from MXene (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2e</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of the superstructured Zn-HMT/MXene are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b,c. After heat treatment at 800 °C in Ar. Zn-HMT/MXene is converted to N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20. The superstructured N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anodes are successfully derived from Zn-HMT/MXene, as evidenced by the XRD patterns. The main crystal phase of the as-formed TiO<sub>2</sub> anode is the rutile phase (PDF: 72-1232), containing a small amount of anatase (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d). The corresponding morphologies of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 after conversion are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e,f. It can be seen that N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 is an obvious layer-by-layer ultrathin composite structure, and it becomes thicker than the preconversion superstructured Zn-HMT/MXene. The inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>g shows a layer-by-layer stacked structure. For comparison, the synthesis and structural characterization of Zn-HMT, mesoporous N/O-C, and superstructured materials with different MXene contents (TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30) are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2–S5</a>, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">To further reveal the more detailed structure of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, we performed further TEM analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a clearly shows that small TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals form a tight carbon matrix. The HRTEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b display a clear lattice fringe with a d-spacing of 0.322 nm, corresponding to the (110) lattice plane of the rutile TiO<sub>2</sub> phase. The SAED pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c) shows good crystallinity of TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals. The element maps (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d–h) show that the C, N, O, and Ti are uniformly distributed in N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20. Based on the specific surface test results in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1 and Figure S6</a>, it was found that TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals embedded in the mesoporous carbon matrix exhibited a large specific surface up to 286.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). Such a large specific surface area may be caused by zinc evaporation.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) TEM images of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; (b) HRTEM images of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; (c) N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 SAED image; (d–h) corresponding element mapping images of C, N, O, and Ti in the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 sample; (i) FTIR spectra; and (j,k) Raman spectra of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The effects of the diffusion of N and O elements at the interface of TiO<sub>2</sub>/C on TiO<sub>2</sub> and C in N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 are further discussed. In the FTIR profile of TiO<sub>2</sub>, the bands at 400–700 cm<sup>–1</sup> are from Ti–O stretching vibrations and Ti–O–Ti stretching vibrations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The peaks located at 1627 and 3423 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to C═C and O–H stretching vibrations, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47 ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i). These peaks also appear in N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30, respectively. Additionally, a broad peak at 1050 cm<sup>–1</sup> related to C–O stretching <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> and a weak peak at 2927 cm<sup>–1</sup> related to C–H vibration <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> are observed for N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30, indicating the formation of carbon layers. In the range of 700–500 cm<sup>–1</sup>, we found some changes in the vibration of Ti–O–Ti <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>i). Compared with the TiO<sub>2</sub> sample peak at 512 cm<sup>–1</sup>, the sharpness of the peak shape of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 has changed, indicating the changes of Ti–O vibrations. While the other two samples have a clear red shift, indicating the effect of the carbon layers on Ti–O–Ti vibrations. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The information on carbon and Ti–O vibrations by means of Raman is further revealed, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>j. The results show that all the peaks below 1000 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to Raman modes of rutile TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> As expected, the E<sub>g</sub> mode at 442 cm<sup>–1</sup> for N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 indicates an obvious red shift as compared to TiO<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>k). The red shift of the E<sub>g</sub> mode may be because of the introduction of oxygen vacancy defects and N atoms in rutile TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The D band at 1339 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the G band at 1595 cm<sup>–1</sup> of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 come from carbon defects and the graphite phase, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> The corresponding <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> values are 1.03, 0.98, and 0.95, respectively. The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of mesoporous carbon obtained by direct pyrolysis is 1.12 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2k</a>), while the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 is 0.98 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>j). Obviously, the ordering degree of mesoporous carbon in superstructure materials was decreased as compared to the mesoporous carbon. The above results show that the interface interaction between TiO<sub>2</sub> and the carbon matrix has an obvious effect on the local structure of TiO<sub>2</sub> and the carbon matrix.</div><div class="NLM_p">The elemental chemical environment of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 samples was analyzed using the XPS spectra, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>. The C 1s peak in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b can be divided into free peaks at 284.8, 285.49, and 288.88 eV, corresponding to C–C, C–O, and O–C═O species, respectively. It is worth noting that N 1s is resolved into four N-based functional groups. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, the peaks at 396.31, 398.44, 399.66, and 400.94 eV are ascribed to the triazine ring (C═N–C), pyridinic CH<sub>2</sub>–NH<sub>2</sub>, pyrrolic or amine moieties N(−NH), and Quaternary-N, respectively, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> indicating that HMT can provide different types of N sources in the as-prepared anodes. Interestingly, this result is also consistent with the chemical environment of N in previous literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XPS survey spectrum; (b) C 1s; (c) N 1s; (d) O 1s; (e) Ti 2p of NC@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; and (f) schematic configuration of N, O-dual-doped carbon based on the high-resolution XPS analysis.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d shows that the O 1s spectra are fitted into four peaks at 530.41, 530.83, 532.32, and 533.75 eV matching well with Ti–O, Ti–O–Ti, C–O, and Ti–O, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> The high-resolution Ti 2p region of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 can be deconvoluted into five peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e). The two peaks at 459.13 and 464.77 eV are attributed to 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Ti<sup>4+</sup>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> While the two weak peaks at 461.25 and 462.80 eV belong to the Ti–C bonding and Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The peak located at 456.87 eV is ascribed to Ti–O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> It can be concluded that N and O have obvious diffusion at the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C interface during the formation of the superstructured anode, which will have a positive impact on the intrinsic conductivity and sodium-storage performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> The N and O double-doped carbon was confirmed by C 1s and N 1s analyses. To some extent, the chemical environment of each element analyzed by XPS reveals the hidden “structure–activity” relationship between the electrode composition and electrochemical properties. To obtain the structural information of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, we have also carried out UV–vis characterization (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> in the Supporting Information).</div><div class="NLM_p">The charge–discharge process of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N/O-C was revealed by CV testing. For the first cycle, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 has an obvious irreversible peak at ∼0.96 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). This is mainly attributed to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer and the decomposition of electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> The CV curves of the four electrodes overlap well in the second and third cycles, indicating that the various anodes exhibit superior electrochemical reversibility. Some symmetrical anode/cathode peaks at 0.09 V for N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and N/O-C electrodes are redox peaks from Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9a–d</a>). Obviously, the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode shows fastest reaction kinetics for Na<sup>+</sup> based on the current density and integral area. It should be emphasized that the irreversible capacity loss in the initial cycle has been a problem to be overcome for SIB anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical performance of the five anodes. (a) CV curves of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode at 0.1 mV<sup>–1</sup>; (b) voltage profile of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrodes at different cycles; (c) rate capability of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 electrodes; (d) rate capability comparison of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode with previously reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based electrodes (see in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>); and (e,f) long cycling performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 anodes at 0.5 and 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The voltage profiles of five anodes are shown at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9e–h</a>, respectively. The first charge–discharge specific capacity of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode material is 359.6 and 154.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Obviously, the first charge–discharge specific capacity of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 is much higher than those of N/O-C, TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9e–h</a>). The superiority of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 was also demonstrated in terms of the rate performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c) and cycling stability (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e,f). For example, for N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, the specific capacitance is 291, 214, 166, and 112 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 returned to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, its specific capacity reached 234 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 outperforms most of the previously reported TiO<sub>2</sub>-based composites in the rate performance (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d), indicating the fast reaction kinetics of the electrode. Surprisingly, the capacity of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 decreases faster than those of other samples with the increase of current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c). This may be related to the changes in the interface structure of the electrodes during charge–discharge, which needs to be further discussed in the future.</div><div class="NLM_p">Long cycling performance of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e,f, respectively. The electrode delivers a discharge capacity of 180 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and a discharge capacity of 165 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> can still be maintained after 1000 cycles, with a Coulombic efficiency near 100% throughout the cycling. The counterparts N/O-C, TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 only delivered lower capacities of 140, 50, 158, and 148 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and 82, 43, 120, and 135 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The superior electrochemical performance of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 is mainly attributed to the synergetic effect of the shortened Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion path, the enhanced surface area, and electronic conductivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">The initial EIS curve of the four electrodes is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. The <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> charge transfer resistance of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 electrodes is calculated to be 42.7, 33, 27.6, and 35.6 Ω, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). Compared with TiO<sub>2</sub>, the R<sub>ct</sub> resistance of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 is decreased. It shows that the carbon matrix has an obvious effect on the rapid electron transport of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based superstructure anodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">The GITT results of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. Surprisingly, the diffusion coefficient of the TiO<sub>2</sub> anode is the highest among the four electrodes, while the diffusion coefficient of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 with the best electrochemical performance is close to those of the other three electrodes. According to sodium-storage electrochemical reaction equation of TiO<sub>2</sub>, to improve the specific capacity and kinetic performance of anodes, it is necessary to enhance the match between the conductivity and the diffusion coefficient of the electrode, except for improving the ion-diffusion coefficient of anodes. Based on the GITT tests, it can be indirectly proved that the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode has more effective active sites and conductivity than the other three electrodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the effect of different TiO<sub>2</sub> contents on the electrochemical performance of anodes, we selected five typical electrodes and tested their cycle performance. The results show that the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode shows high specific capacity and good cycling stability regardless of low current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e) or high current density (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f). For example, the specific capacity of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode is stable at 165 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, while the capacities of TiO<sub>2</sub> and N/O-C electrodes are 43 and 82 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. According to the content of TiO<sub>2</sub> and the carbon matrix in the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), we made a further analysis of the capacity contribution of TiO<sub>2</sub> and the carbon matrix in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. The results show that the synergistic effect of TiO<sub>2</sub> and the carbon matrix in the superstructure indicates the effect of 1 + 1 &gt; 2. To reveal its sodium-storage behavior, it is necessary to further explore the kinetic mechanism behind the improvement of its electrochemical performance from different perspectives.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b shows the CV curves of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrodes at different scan rates, ranging from 0.4 to 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a, the redox peak of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode is more obvious than those of TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30, and the peak current is higher (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a,d,g</a>), indicating superior Na<sup>+</sup>-storage kinetic properties. The results of the total pseudocapacitance contribution of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode are based on <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>. We can quantitatively obtain the contribution of pseudocapacitance (<i>K</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and intercalation (<i>K</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e shows that the pseudocapacitance fraction of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode at a scan rate of 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup> is 33.9%, while the pseudocapacitance percentages of the TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 electrodes are 43.1, 25.7, 42.7, and 29.7% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S12b,e,h</a>), respectively. For the performance comparison and analysis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>), we refer to the work of pseudocapacitive TiO<sub>2</sub> and carbon anodes in recent years. Most of the reported pseudocapacitive proportion values of anodes account for more than 50%. Our experimental results, especially the proportion of pseudocapacitance, are lower than most of the reported values in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 23 and 24">(23,24)</a> Therefore, we have made a special comparative analysis, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g. For example, the pseudocapacitance of TiO<sub>2</sub> itself is the highest (43.1%) among the four anodes, while the pseudocapacitance of the mesoporous carbon layer is the lowest (25.7%). We believe that the differences in ion diffusion and electron transport between traditional structures and the superstructure itself lead to abnormal results. From the microstructure analysis of the whole composite anodes and the pseudocapacitance mechanism of TiO<sub>2</sub> itself, the sodium storage of superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub>/C anodes mainly includes three parts (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h): surface sodium storage, interface sodium storage, and bulk sodium storage. The corresponding capacity analysis is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i. Based on the above results, we believe that the interface between the mesoporous superstructured carbon layer and TiO<sub>2</sub> plays an important role in pseudocapacitance enhancement. Compared with most of the TiO<sub>2</sub>/C composites in the previous literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> the pseudocapacitance proportion of superstructured anodes is less than 50% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g). The results show that this unique superstructure is conducive to increasing the ion-diffusion capacity.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a,d) CV curves of TiO<sub>2</sub> and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrodes at different scan rates; (b,e) pseudocapacitive contribution (red and pink region) to the total current of TiO<sub>2</sub> and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 at a scan rate of 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (c,f) percentage of capacitive contributions obtained at different scan rates of TiO<sub>2</sub> and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 at scan rates of 0.4, 0.8, 2.0, 4.0, 8.0, and 10 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; the analysis of pseudocapacitance of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; and (g) comparison of pseudocapacitance contribution of different anodes. (h,i) Surface sodium storage (I), interfacial sodium storage (II), and bulk sodium storage (III).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The peak current, peak position, peak symmetry, and peak separation with the scan rate of the CV curves from electrodes at different scanning rates can reflect the electrochemical behavior of electrodes. This key information satisfies the following two equations. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="Eq 3">Eq 3</a> is derived from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i13"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i14"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></span></div><div class="NLM_p">The <i>b</i> value in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a> can provide information on whether the electrode is diffusion-controlled (<i>b</i> value close to 0.5) or surface-controlled (<i>b</i> value close to 1). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a> shows that the <i>b</i> values of N/O-C, TiO<sub>2</sub>, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 are 0.5329, 0.5476, 0.5458, 0.6008, and 0.5946, respectively. It can be inferred that the sodium-storage behavior of the above four samples is mainly controlled by diffusion rather than the surface at a high scanning rate. Although this conclusion is contrary to the trend of most literature, it raises a problem for us, that is, how to improve the electrode diffusion kinetics at a high scanning rate. Because TiO<sub>2</sub> itself is a pseudocapacitive material, it is an industry consensus that the electrode surface can quickly store charges at a high scanning rate. However, we have obtained the charge storage behavior dominated by electrode diffusion at a high scanning rate due to the unique superstructured anodes. The corresponding Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism needs to be further studied in the future.</div><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the changes of the crystal structure and morphologies of different anodes after cycles, we collected XRD and FESEM data of four electrodes after 100 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>. From the comparative analysis of XRD patterns, the new crystal phases generated are almost the same after 100 cycles of each anode. This may be related to the TiO<sub>2</sub> structure itself. For example, new crystalline phases Na<sub>8</sub>Ti<sub>4</sub>O<sub>8</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> have been produced. In the view of crystallinity, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 has worst crystallinity than the other three, which is understandable because of the confinement effect of carbon during the formation of TiO<sub>2</sub>. This can also be concluded in their corresponding FESEM, where large particles are rarely observed at N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20. The other three electrodes with large particles are displayed in yellow. Among the three kinds of N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 electrodes, N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10 and N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 did not form a stacked layer-by-layer superstructured TiO<sub>2</sub>@C, thus affecting the poor structural stability and reversible capacity of the other two anodes.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. XRD and corresponding SEM images of TiO<sub>2</sub>, NC@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10, NC@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20, and NC@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30 after 100 cycles at a current density of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (a) XRD patterns. FESEM images of (b) N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-30; (c) N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20; (d) N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-10; and (e) TiO<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To reveal the mechanism of sodium-ion insertion/extraction from N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode, we selected three points according to the initial Na<sup>+</sup> charge–discharge profile (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a). The corresponding ex situ XRD patterns of anodes at different stages is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b. It can be seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b that the irreversibility of the first charge and discharge is mainly due to the formation of Na<sub>8</sub>Ti<sub>4</sub>O<sub>8</sub>. The other diffraction peaks and the original anode are almost the same except for a small shift (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>c), indicating the stability of the electrode structure during the charge–discharge process. Besides the generated Na<sub>8</sub>Ti<sub>4</sub>O<sub>8</sub>, the irreversibility of anodes is also partly caused by the SEI film of the anode surface. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a></div><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/medium/ic2c01009_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) First charge–discharge profile of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode; (b,c) ex situ XRD patterns of the N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 electrode at different charge–discharge stages; and (d) schematic illustration of the specific capacity contribution of mesoporous carbon and TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals in the superstructured N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/asset/images/large/ic2c01009_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The superstructured N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anodes included three important Na<sup>+</sup> storage sites: TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals, mesoporous N/O codoped carbon, and their covalent coupling interface. The mesoporous carbon matrix plays the Na<sup>+</sup>-storage role, while N/O codoped carbon matrix undertakes the dual tasks of electron transport and ion storage. The incorporation of N and O atoms into the carbon framework can effectively adjust the electronic structure of the surrounding carbon atoms, regulate the local charge density distribution, leading to the improvement of the Na<sup>+</sup> chemical reactivity in the carbon matrix, and then improve its energy storage capacity. On the other hand, the nanocrystals in the confined space of the carbon matrix have good Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity because of their pseudocapacitance properties. According to the previous XRD, Raman, and XPS results, there is a covalent coupling at the interface of the superstructured N/O-C@TiO<sub>2</sub>-20 anodes, accompanied by the generation of oxygen vacancies. The effect of oxygen vacancies and covalent interface coupling on the performance of the electrodes has been reported. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref60" aria-label="reference 23, 24 and 60">(23,24,60)</a></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i15">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, using MXene as a symbiotic template, the fabrication of 2D TiO<sub>2</sub>/C superstructures was achieved through surface electrostatic adsorption and interlayer coassembly strategies. O and Zn sources provided by nitrates, and C and N sources provided by HMT are prerequisites of the mesoporous N/O double-doped carbon matrix. The MXene-derived TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals are confined in the carbon matrix, ensuring their uniform dispersion. Due to the unique structure, the superior electrochemical performance is finally obtained by the optimized superstructured anode. This work not only provides an effective method for the assembly and regulation of superstructure anodes to achieve high-performance sodium-ion batteries but also deepens the understanding of the role of different compositions in the superstructure in energy storage, so as to provide advanced materials and reveal their unique Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanism.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Few-layered MXene synthesis; synthesis and characterizations of N/O; dual-doped carbon; formation of superstructured Zn-HMT/TisC<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>-20; XRD patterns and FESEM images of superstructured Zn-HMT/TisC<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub>-X and N/O-C@ TiO<sub>2</sub>-X; BET isotherms, UV–vis spectra, and TG-DTG, CV, and voltage profile curves; EIS and GITT data; CV curves and pseudocapacitive contribution; logarithm peak current versus logarithm scan rate plots; specific surface area; total pore volume and average pore size; electrochemical data derived from EIS; and a summary of electrochemical performance of TiO<sub>2</sub>-based anodes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009/suppl_file/ic2c01009_si_001.pdf">ic2c01009_si_001.pdf (2.11 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.inorgchem.2c01009" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Unified picture of anionic redox in Li/Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ben Yahia, Mouna; Vergnet, Jean; Saubanere, Matthieu; Doublet, Marie-Liesse</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">18</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">496-502</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Anionic redox in Li-rich and Na-rich transition metal oxides (A-rich-TMOs) has emerged as a new paradigm to increase the energy d. of rechargeable batteries.  Ever since, numerous electrodes delivering extra anionic capacity beyond the theor. cationic capacity have been reported.  Unfortunately, most often the anionic capacity achieved in charge is partly irreversible in discharge.  A unified picture of anionic redox in A-rich-TMOs is designed here to identify the electronic origin of this irreversibility and to propose new directions to improve the cycling performance of the electrodes.  The electron localization function is introduced as a holistic tool to unambiguously locate the O lone pairs in the structure and follow their participation in the redox activity of A-rich-TMOs.  The charge-transfer gap of transition metal oxides is proposed as the pertinent observable to quantify the amt. of extra capacity achievable in charge and its reversibility in discharge, irresp. of the material chem. compn.  From this generalized approach, the reversibility of the anionic capacity is limited to a crit. no. of O holes per O, hO ≤ 1/3.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpNxw9BVJtqRbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgq8SZD6Sn3Xw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1MXmslans7Y%253D&amp;md5=7a52af474fdbf2a6e1b7b38d0addd2bb</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A cost and resource analysis of sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational design of layered oxide materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhao, Chenglong; Wang, Qidi; Yao, Zhenpeng; Wang, Jianlin; Sanchez-Lengeling, Benjamin; Ding, Feixiang; Qi, Xingguo; Lu, Yaxiang; Bai, Xuedong; Li, Baohua; Li, Hong; Aspuru-Guzik, Alan; Huang, Xuejie; Delmas, Claude; Wagemaker, Marnix; Chen, Liquan; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">370</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6517</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">708-711</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SCIEAS</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1095-9203</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Association for the Advancement of Science</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have captured widespread attention for grid-scale energy storage owing to the natural abundance of sodium.  The performance of such batteries is limited by available electrode materials, esp. for sodium-ion layered oxides, motivating the exploration of high compositional diversity.  How the compn. dets. the structural chem. is decisive for the electrochem. performance but very challenging to predict, esp. for complex compns.  The "cationic potential" is introduced that captures the key interactions of layered materials and makes it possible to predict the stacking structures.  This is demonstrated through the rational design and prepn. of layered electrode materials with improved performance.  As the stacking structure dets. the functional properties, this methodol. offers a soln. toward the design of alkali metal layered oxides.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqSJI5LmdJYXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhAyiuL_XuUpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXit1Kru7fI&amp;md5=979fa3c7f742539bf50f3e5dea98b16a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.202001852"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J. Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S. X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electron Delocalization and Dissolution-Restraint in Vanadium Oxide Superlattices to Boost Electrochemical Performance of Aqueous Zinc-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electron Delocalization and Dissolution-Restraint in Vanadium Oxide Superlattices to Boost Electrochemical Performance of Aqueous Zinc-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Weijie; Han, Chao; Gu, Qinfen; Chou, Shu-Lei; Wang, Jia-Zhao; Liu, Hua-Kun; Dou, Shi-Xue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
